			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Richard II of England</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Richard II</b> (6 January 1367 – c. 14 February 1400), also known as <b>Richard of Bordeaux</b>, was <a href="/wiki/King_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="King of England">King of England</a> from 1377 until he was <a href="/wiki/Deposed" class="mw-redirect" title="Deposed">deposed</a> on 30 September 1399. Richard, a son of <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a>, was born in <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a> during the reign of his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward III">Edward III</a>. His father was Prince of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>. Richard was the younger brother of <a href="/wiki/Edward_of_Angoul%C3%AAme" title="Edward of Angoulême">Edward of Angoulême</a>, upon whose death Richard, at three years of age, became second in line to the throne after his father. Upon the death of Richard's father prior to the death of Edward III, Richard, by <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>, became the <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the throne. With Edward III's death the following year, Richard succeeded to the throne at the age of ten.</p>
<p>During Richard's first years as king, government was in the hands of a series of councils. Most of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_British_peerage#Feudal_origins" title="History of the British peerage">aristocracy</a> preferred this to a <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regency</a> led by the king's uncle, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt" title="John of Gaunt">John of Gaunt</a>, yet Gaunt remained highly influential. The first major challenge of the reign was the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">Peasants' Revolt</a> in 1381. The young king played a major part in the successful suppression of this crisis. In the following years, however, the king's dependence on a small number of courtiers caused discontent among the influential, and in 1387 control of government was taken over by a group of aristocrats known as the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a>. By 1389 Richard had regained control, and for the next eight years governed in relative harmony with his former opponents.</p>
<p>In 1397, Richard took his revenge on the appellants, many of whom were executed or exiled. The next two years have been described by historians as Richard's "tyranny". In 1399, after John of Gaunt died, the king disinherited Gaunt's son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry of Bolingbroke</a>, who had previously been exiled. Henry invaded England in June 1399 with a small force that quickly grew in numbers. Although he initially claimed that his goal was only to reclaim his patrimony, it soon became clear that Henry intended to claim the throne for himself. Meeting little resistance, Bolingbroke deposed Richard and had himself crowned as King Henry IV. Richard died in captivity in February 1400; he is thought to have been starved to death, although questions remain regarding his final fate.</p>
<p>Richard was said to have been tall, good-looking and intelligent. Less warlike than either his father or grandfather, he sought to bring an end to the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a> that Edward III had started. He was a firm believer in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>, which led him to restrain the power of the aristocracy, and to rely on a private <a href="/wiki/Retinue" title="Retinue">retinue</a> for military protection instead; in contrast to the fraternal, martial court of his grandfather, he cultivated a refined atmosphere at his court, in which the king was an elevated figure, with art and culture at its centre.</p>
<p>Richard's posthumous reputation has been shaped to a large extent by <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, whose play <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">Richard II</a></i> portrayed Richard's misrule and his deposition by Bolingbroke as responsible for the 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>. Modern historians do not accept this interpretation, while not exonerating Richard from responsibility for his own deposition. While probably not insane, as historians of the 19th and 20th centuries believed, he may have had what psychologists today identify as a <a href="/wiki/Personality_disorder" title="Personality disorder">personality disorder</a>, particularly manifesting itself towards the end of his reign. Most authorities agree that, even though his policies were not unprecedented or entirely unrealistic, the way in which he carried them out was unacceptable to the political establishment, and this led to his downfall.</p>
<p></p>
