<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/220px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" width="220" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/330px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg/440px-Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg 2x" data-file-width="771" data-file-height="889" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_Black_Prince_14thc.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
14th-century manuscript <a href="/wiki/Historiated_initial" title="Historiated initial">historiated initial</a> showing <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a> kneeling before his father, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Richard of Bordeaux was the younger son of <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Kent" title="Joan of Kent">Joan of Kent</a>. Edward, <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the throne of England, had distinguished himself as a military commander in the early phases of the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Poitiers" title="Battle of Poitiers">Battle of Poitiers</a> in 1356. After further military adventures, however, he contracted <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a> in Spain in 1370. He never fully recovered and had to return to England the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Joan of Kent had been at the centre of a marriage dispute between <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Holland,_1st_Earl_of_Kent" title="Thomas Holland, 1st Earl of Kent">Thomas Holland, Earl of Kent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Montacute,_2nd_Earl_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="William Montacute, 2nd Earl of Salisbury">William Montacute, 2nd Earl of Salisbury</a>, from which Holland emerged victorious. Less than a year after Holland's death in 1360, Joan married Prince Edward. Since Joan was a granddaughter of <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_I" class="mw-redirect" title="King Edward I">King Edward I</a> and a first cousin of her father-in-law, King Edward III, the marriage required <a href="/wiki/Dispensation_(canon_law)#Matrimonial_dispensation" title="Dispensation (canon law)">papal approval</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard was born at the Archbishop's Palace, <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux" title="Bordeaux">Bordeaux</a>, in the English principality of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine#Hundred_Years.27_War" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>, on 6 January 1367. According to contemporary sources, three kings&#160;– "the King of <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">Castille</a>, the King of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a> and the King of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>"&#160;– were present at his birth.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> This anecdote, and the fact that his birth fell on the feast of <a href="/wiki/Epiphany_(holiday)" title="Epiphany (holiday)">Epiphany</a>, was later used in the religious imagery of the <a href="/wiki/Wilton_Diptych" title="Wilton Diptych">Wilton Diptych</a>, where Richard is one of three kings paying homage to the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_and_Child" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin and Child">Virgin and Child</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> His elder brother Edward of Angoulême died near his sixth birthday in 1371, and Richard became his father's heir.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The Black Prince finally succumbed to his long illness in June 1376. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">Commons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">parliament</a> genuinely feared that Richard's uncle, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt" title="John of Gaunt">John of Gaunt</a>, would <a href="/wiki/Usurp" class="mw-redirect" title="Usurp">usurp</a> the throne.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Anone"><a href="#endnote_Anone">[a]</a></sup> For this reason, the prince was quickly invested with the <a href="/wiki/Princedom_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Princedom of Wales">princedom of Wales</a> and his father's other titles.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux,_Prince_of_Wales_(later_Richard_II).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg/100px-Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg.png" width="100" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg/150px-Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg/200px-Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux%2C_Prince_of_Wales_%28later_Richard_II%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="478" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Richard_of_Bordeaux,_Prince_of_Wales_(later_Richard_II).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arms of<br />
Richard of Bordeaux,<br />
Prince of Wales<br />
(later Richard II)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 21 June the next year, Richard's grandfather <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> also died, and the 10-year-old Richard was <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_British_monarch" title="Coronation of the British monarch">crowned king</a> on 16 July 1377.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Again, fears of John of Gaunt's ambitions influenced political decisions, and a regency led by the King's uncles was avoided.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Instead, the king was nominally to exercise kingship with the help of a series of "continual councils," from which John of Gaunt was excluded.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Gaunt, together with his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Thomas_of_Woodstock,_1st_Duke_of_Gloucester" title="Thomas of Woodstock, 1st Duke of Gloucester">Thomas of Woodstock, Earl of Buckingham</a>, still held great informal influence over the business of government, but the king's councillors and friends, particularly Sir <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Burley" title="Simon de Burley">Simon de Burley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_de_Vere,_Duke_of_Ireland" title="Robert de Vere, Duke of Ireland">Robert de Vere, Duke of Ireland</a>, increasingly gained control of royal affairs. In a matter of three years, these councillors earned the mistrust of the Commons to the point that the councils were discontinued in 1380.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Contributing to discontent was an increasingly heavy burden of <a href="/wiki/Taxation_in_medieval_England" title="Taxation in medieval England">taxation</a> levied through three <a href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax per head">poll taxes</a> between 1377 and 1381 that were spent on unsuccessful military expeditions on the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> By 1381, there was a deep-felt resentment against the governing classes in the lower levels of English society.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Peasants'_Revolt"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Peasants.27_Revolt">Peasants' Revolt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Peasants' Revolt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Whereas the poll tax of 1381 was the spark of the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">Peasants' Revolt</a>, the root of the conflict lay in tensions between peasants and landowners precipitated by the economic and demographic consequences of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death_in_England" title="Black Death in England">Black Death</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_plague_pandemic" title="Second plague pandemic">subsequent outbreaks</a> of the plague.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> The rebellion started in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Essex" title="Essex">Essex</a> in late May, and on 12 June, bands of peasants gathered at <a href="/wiki/Blackheath,_London" title="Blackheath, London">Blackheath</a> near London under the leaders <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">Wat Tyler</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Ball_(priest)" title="John Ball (priest)">John Ball</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Straw_(rebel_leader)" title="Jack Straw (rebel leader)">Jack Straw</a>. John of Gaunt's <a href="/wiki/Savoy_Palace" title="Savoy Palace">Savoy Palace</a> was burnt down. The <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> <a href="/wiki/Simon_Sudbury" title="Simon Sudbury">Simon Sudbury</a>, who was also <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a>, and the king's <a href="/wiki/Lord_High_Treasurer" title="Lord High Treasurer">Lord High Treasurer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hales" title="Robert Hales">Robert Hales</a>, were both killed by the rebels,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> who were demanding the complete abolition of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-H231_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H231-12">[12]</a></sup> The king, sheltered within the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> with his councillors, agreed that the Crown did not have the forces to disperse the rebels and that the only feasible option was to negotiate.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg/220px-Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg/330px-Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg/440px-Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1459" data-file-height="1233" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_of_Wat_Tyler_Froissart.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Richard II watches <a href="/wiki/Wat_Tyler" title="Wat Tyler">Wat Tyler</a>'s death and addresses the peasants in the background: taken from the <a href="/wiki/Froissart_of_Louis_of_Gruuthuse_(BnF_Fr_2643-6)" title="Froissart of Louis of Gruuthuse (BnF Fr 2643-6)">Gruuthuse manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Froissart" title="Jean Froissart">Froissart</a>'s <i>Chroniques</i> (c.&#160;1475).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>It is unclear how much Richard, who was still only fourteen years old, was involved in these deliberations, although historians have suggested that he was among the proponents of negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> The king set out by the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a> on 13 June, but the large number of people thronging the banks at <a href="/wiki/Greenwich" title="Greenwich">Greenwich</a> made it impossible for him to land, forcing him to return to the Tower.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The next day, Friday, 14 June, he set out by horse and met the rebels at <a href="/wiki/Mile_End" title="Mile End">Mile End</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The king agreed to the rebels' demands, but this move only emboldened them; they continued their looting and killings.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Richard met Wat Tyler again the next day at <a href="/wiki/Smithfield,_London" title="Smithfield, London">Smithfield</a> and reiterated that the demands would be met, but the rebel leader was not convinced of the king's sincerity. The king's men grew restive, an altercation broke out, and <a href="/wiki/William_Walworth" title="William Walworth">William Walworth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_the_City_of_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mayor of the City of London">mayor of London</a>, pulled Tyler down from his horse and killed him.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The situation became tense once the rebels realised what had happened, but the king acted with calm resolve and, saying "I am your captain, follow me!", he led the mob away from the scene.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Bnone"><a href="#endnote_Bnone">[b]</a></sup> Walworth meanwhile gathered a force to surround the peasant army, but the king granted clemency and allowed the rebels to disperse and return to their homes.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The king soon revoked the charters of freedom and pardon that he had granted, and as disturbances continued in other parts of the country, he personally went into Essex to suppress the rebellion. On 28 June at <a href="/wiki/Billericay" title="Billericay">Billericay</a>, he defeated the last rebels in a small skirmish and effectively ended the Peasants' Revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-H231_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-H231-12">[12]</a></sup> Despite his young age, Richard had shown great courage and determination in his handling of the rebellion. It is likely, though, that the events impressed upon him the dangers of disobedience and threats to royal authority, and helped shape the <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolutist</a> attitudes to kingship that would later prove fatal to his reign.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coming_of_age">Coming of age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coming of age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>It is only with the Peasants' Revolt that Richard starts to emerge clearly in the <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">annals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> One of his first significant acts after the rebellion was to marry <a href="/wiki/Anne_of_Bohemia" title="Anne of Bohemia">Anne of Bohemia</a>, daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> (<a href="/wiki/King_of_Bohemia" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Bohemia">King of Bohemia</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a>) and his wife <a href="/wiki/Elisabeth_of_Pomerania" class="mw-redirect" title="Elisabeth of Pomerania">Elisabeth of Pomerania</a>, on 20 January 1382.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The marriage had diplomatic significance; in the division of Europe caused by the <a href="/wiki/Western_Schism" title="Western Schism">Western Schism</a>, Bohemia and the Empire were seen as potential allies against <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="France in the Middle Ages">France</a> in the ongoing <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Cnone"><a href="#endnote_Cnone">[c]</a></sup> Nonetheless, the marriage was not popular in England. Despite great sums of money awarded to the Empire, the political alliance never resulted in any military victories.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Furthermore, the marriage was childless. Anne died from plague in 1394, greatly mourned by her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_de_la_Pole,_1st_Earl_of_Suffolk" title="Michael de la Pole, 1st Earl of Suffolk">Michael de la Pole</a> had been instrumental in the marriage negotiations;<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> he had the king's confidence and gradually became more involved at court and in government as Richard came of age.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> De la Pole came from an upstart merchant family.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> When Richard made him <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">chancellor</a> in 1383, and created him <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Suffolk" title="Earl of Suffolk">Earl of Suffolk</a> two years later, this antagonised the more established nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Another member of the close circle around the king was <a href="/wiki/Robert_de_Vere,_Duke_of_Ireland" title="Robert de Vere, Duke of Ireland">Robert de Vere, Earl of Oxford</a>, who in this period emerged as the king's <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Richard's close friendship to de Vere was also disagreeable to the political establishment. This displeasure was exacerbated by the earl's elevation to the new title of <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Ireland" title="Duke of Ireland">Duke of Ireland</a> in 1386.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The chronicler <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walsingham" title="Thomas Walsingham">Thomas Walsingham</a> suggested the relationship between the king and de Vere was of a homosexual nature, due to a resentment Walsingham had toward the king.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tensions came to a head over the approach to the war in France. While the court party preferred negotiations, Gaunt and Buckingham urged a large-scale campaign to protect English possessions.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Instead, a so-called <a href="/wiki/Crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusade">crusade</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Henry_le_Despenser" title="Henry le Despenser">Henry le Despenser</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Norwich" title="Bishop of Norwich">Bishop of Norwich</a>, was dispatched, which failed miserably.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Faced with this setback on the continent, Richard turned his attention instead towards France's ally, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a>. In 1385, the king himself led a <a href="/wiki/Punitive_expedition" title="Punitive expedition">punitive expedition</a> to the north,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> but the effort came to nothing, and the army had to return without ever engaging the Scots in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Meanwhile, only an uprising in <a href="/wiki/Ghent" title="Ghent">Ghent</a> prevented a French invasion of southern England.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The relationship between Richard and his uncle John of Gaunt deteriorated further with military failure, and John of Gaunt left England to pursue his claim to the throne of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a> in 1386 amid rumours of a plot against his person.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> With Gaunt gone, the unofficial leadership of the growing dissent against the king and his courtiers passed to Buckingham&#160;– who had by now been created Duke of Gloucester&#160;– and <a href="/wiki/Richard_FitzAlan,_11th_Earl_of_Arundel" title="Richard FitzAlan, 11th Earl of Arundel">Richard Fitzalan, Earl of Arundel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="First_crisis_of_1386–88"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_crisis_of_1386.E2.80.9388">First crisis of 1386–88</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: First crisis of 1386–88">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg/220px-Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg/330px-Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg/440px-Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg 2x" data-file-width="923" data-file-height="689" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_de_Vere_fleeing_Radcot_Bridge.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Robert_de_Vere,_Duke_of_Ireland" title="Robert de Vere, Duke of Ireland">Robert de Vere</a> fleeing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Radcot_Bridge" title="Battle of Radcot Bridge">Radcot Bridge</a> (Froissart)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The threat of a French invasion did not subside, but instead grew stronger into 1386.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> At the parliament of October that year, Michael de la Pole&#160;– in his capacity of chancellor&#160;– requested taxation of an unprecedented level for the defence of the realm.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Rather than consenting, the parliament responded by refusing to consider any request until the chancellor was removed.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The parliament (later known as the <a href="/wiki/Wonderful_Parliament" title="Wonderful Parliament">Wonderful Parliament</a>) was presumably working with the support of Gloucester and Arundel.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The king famously responded that he would not dismiss as much as a <a href="/wiki/Scullery_maid" title="Scullery maid">scullion</a> from his kitchen at parliament's request.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Only when threatened with deposition was Richard forced to give in and let de la Pole go.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A commission was set up to review and control royal finances for a year.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard was deeply perturbed by this affront to his royal prerogative, and from February to November 1387 went on a "gyration" (tour) of the country to muster support for his cause.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> By installing de Vere as <a href="/wiki/Justice_of_Chester" title="Justice of Chester">Justice of Chester</a>, he began the work of creating a loyal military power base in <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> He also secured a legal ruling from <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chief_Justice_of_England_and_Wales" title="Lord Chief Justice of England and Wales">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tresilian" title="Robert Tresilian">Robert Tresilian</a> that parliament's conduct had been unlawful and treasonable.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>On his return to London, the king was confronted by Thomas of Woodstock (now Duke of Gloucester), Arundel and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Beauchamp,_12th_Earl_of_Warwick" title="Thomas de Beauchamp, 12th Earl of Warwick">Thomas de Beauchamp, Earl of Warwick</a>, who brought an appeal<sup class="reference" id="ref_Dnone"><a href="#endnote_Dnone">[d]</a></sup> of treason against de la Pole, de Vere, Tresilian, and two other loyalists: the mayor of London, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brembre" title="Nicholas Brembre">Nicholas Brembre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Neville" title="Alexander Neville">Alexander Neville</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Richard stalled the negotiations to gain time, as he was expecting de Vere to arrive from Cheshire with military reinforcements.<sup id="cite_ref-S187_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S187-42">[42]</a></sup> The three earls then joined forces with <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry Bolingbroke, Earl of Derby</a> (Gaunt's son, later King <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Mowbray,_1st_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas de Mowbray, 1st Duke of Norfolk">Thomas de Mowbray, Earl of Nottingham</a>&#160;– the group known to history as the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a>. On 20 December 1387 they intercepted de Vere at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Radcot_Bridge" title="Battle of Radcot Bridge">Radcot Bridge</a>, where he and his forces were routed and he was obliged to flee the country.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard now had no choice but to comply with the appellants' demands; Brembre and Tresilian were condemned and executed, while de Vere and de la Pole&#160;– who had by now also left the country<sup id="cite_ref-S187_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S187-42">[42]</a></sup>&#160;– were sentenced to death <i>in absentia</i> at the <a href="/wiki/Merciless_Parliament" title="Merciless Parliament">Merciless Parliament</a> in February 1388.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The proceedings went further, and a number of Richard's chamber knights were also executed, among these Burley.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The appellants had now succeeded completely in breaking up the circle of favourites around the king.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_fragile_peace">A fragile peace</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: A fragile peace">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_(1377-1399).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg/220px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg.png" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg/330px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg/440px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_%281377-1399%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1550" data-file-height="1450" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Richard_II_of_England_(1377-1399).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coat of arms of King Richard II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Richard gradually re-established royal authority in the months after the deliberations of the Merciless Parliament. The aggressive foreign policy of the Lords Appellant failed when their efforts to build a wide, anti-French coalition came to nothing, and the north of England fell victim to a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Otterburn" title="Battle of Otterburn">Scottish incursion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Richard was now over twenty-one years old and could with confidence claim the right to govern in his own name.<sup id="cite_ref-S203-4_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S203-4-47">[47]</a></sup> Furthermore, John of Gaunt returned to England in 1389 and settled his differences with the king, after which the old statesman acted as a moderating influence on English politics.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Richard assumed full control of the government on 3 May 1389, claiming that the difficulties of the past years had been due solely to bad councillors. He outlined a foreign policy that reversed the actions of the appellants by seeking peace and reconciliation with France, and promised to lessen the burden of taxation on the people significantly.<sup id="cite_ref-S203-4_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S203-4-47">[47]</a></sup> Richard ruled peacefully for the next eight years, having reconciled with his former adversaries.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Still, later events would show that he had not forgotten the indignities he perceived.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> In particular, the execution of his former teacher Sir Simon de Burley was an insult not easily forgotten.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>With national stability secured, Richard began negotiating a permanent peace with France. A proposal put forward in 1393 would have greatly expanded the territory of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a> possessed by the English crown. However, the plan failed because it included a requirement that the English king pay <a href="/wiki/Homage_(medieval)" class="mw-redirect" title="Homage (medieval)">homage</a> to the King of France&#160;– a condition that proved unacceptable to the English public.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Instead, in 1396, a truce was agreed to, which was to last 28 years.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> As part of the truce, Richard agreed to marry <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Valois" title="Isabella of Valois">Isabella</a>, daughter of <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI_of_France" title="Charles VI of France">Charles VI of France</a>, when she came of age. There were some misgivings about the betrothal, in particular because the princess was then only six years old, and thus would not be able to produce an heir to the throne of England for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_halfpenny_of_(Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578)_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg/220px-Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg" width="220" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg/330px-Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg/440px-Silver_halfpenny_of_%28Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578%29_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2166" data-file-height="2064" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Silver_halfpenny_of_(Richard_II_YORYM_1980_1578)_obverse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Silver half penny of Richard II, <a href="/wiki/York_Museums_Trust" title="York Museums Trust">York Museums Trust</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Richard sought peace with France, he took a different approach to the situation in Ireland. The English <a href="/wiki/Lordship_of_Ireland" title="Lordship of Ireland">lordships in Ireland</a> were in danger of being overrun, and the <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Norman" class="mw-redirect" title="Hiberno-Norman">Anglo-Irish</a> lords were pleading for the king to intervene.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the autumn of 1394, Richard left for Ireland, where he remained until May 1395. His army of more than 8,000 men was the largest force brought to the island during the late Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The invasion was a success, and a number of Irish chieftains submitted to English overlordship.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> It was one of the most successful achievements of Richard's reign, and strengthened the king's support at home, although the consolidation of the English position in Ireland proved to be short-lived.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Second_crisis_of_1397–99"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_crisis_of_1397.E2.80.9399">Second crisis of 1397–99</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Second crisis of 1397–99">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The period that historians refer to as the "tyranny" of Richard II began towards the end of the 1390s.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The king had Gloucester, Arundel and Warwick arrested in July 1397. The timing of these arrests and Richard's motivation are not entirely clear. Although one chronicle suggested that a plot was being planned against the king, there is no evidence that this was the case.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It is more likely that Richard had simply come to feel strong enough to safely retaliate against these three men for their role in events of 1386–88 and eliminate them as threats to his power.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Arundel was the first of the three to be brought to trial, at the parliament of September 1397. After a heated quarrel with the king, he was condemned and executed.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Gloucester was being held prisoner by the Earl of Nottingham at Calais while awaiting his trial. As the time for the trial drew near, Nottingham brought news that Gloucester was dead. It is thought likely that the king had ordered him to be killed to avoid the disgrace of executing a prince of the blood.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Warwick was also condemned to death, but his life was spared and his sentence reduced to life imprisonment. Arundel's brother <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Arundel" title="Thomas Arundel">Thomas Arundel</a>, the Archbishop of Canterbury, was exiled for life.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Richard then took his persecution of adversaries to the localities. While recruiting <a href="/wiki/Retinue" title="Retinue">retainers</a> for himself in various counties, he prosecuted local men who had been loyal to the appellants. The fines levied on these men brought great revenues to the crown, although contemporary chroniclers raised questions about the legality of the proceedings.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnofgaunt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johnofgaunt.jpg/170px-Johnofgaunt.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johnofgaunt.jpg/255px-Johnofgaunt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Johnofgaunt.jpg/340px-Johnofgaunt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="431" data-file-height="550" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Johnofgaunt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/John_of_Gaunt,_1st_Duke_of_Lancaster" class="mw-redirect" title="John of Gaunt, 1st Duke of Lancaster">John of Gaunt</a> had been at the centre of English politics for over thirty years, and his death in 1399 led to insecurity.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These actions were made possible primarily through the collusion of John of Gaunt, but with the support of a large group of other magnates, many of whom were rewarded with new titles, who were disparagingly referred to as Richard's "duketti".<sup id="cite_ref-Saul_.282005.29.2C_p._63._63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saul_.282005.29.2C_p._63.-63">[63]</a></sup> These included the former Appellants <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry Bolingbroke, Earl of Derby</a>, who was made <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Hereford" title="Duke of Hereford">Duke of Hereford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Mowbray,_1st_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas de Mowbray, 1st Duke of Norfolk">Thomas de Mowbray, Earl of Nottingham</a>, who was created <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Duke of Norfolk">Duke of Norfolk</a>. Also among them were <a href="/wiki/John_Holland,_1st_Duke_of_Exeter" title="John Holland, 1st Duke of Exeter">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Holland,_1st_Duke_of_Surrey" title="Thomas Holland, 1st Duke of Surrey">Thomas Holland</a>, the king's half-brother and nephew, who were promoted from earls of <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Huntingdon" title="Earl of Huntingdon">Huntingdon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Kent" title="Earl of Kent">Kent</a> to dukes of <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Exeter" title="Duke of Exeter">Exeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Surrey" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Surrey">Surrey</a> respectively; the Duke of York's son <a href="/wiki/Edward_of_Norwich,_2nd_Duke_of_York" title="Edward of Norwich, 2nd Duke of York">Edward, Earl of Rutland</a>, who received Gloucester's French title of <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Aumale" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke of Aumale">Duke of Aumale</a>; Gaunt's son <a href="/wiki/John_Beaufort,_1st_Earl_of_Somerset" title="John Beaufort, 1st Earl of Somerset">John Beaufort, Earl of Somerset</a>, who was made <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Somerset" class="mw-redirect" title="Marquess of Somerset">Marquess of Somerset</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Dorset" title="Marquess of Dorset">Marquess of Dorset</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_Montacute,_3rd_Earl_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="John Montacute, 3rd Earl of Salisbury">John Montacute, Earl of Salisbury</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_le_Despenser,_1st_Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Thomas le Despenser, 1st Earl of Gloucester">Lord Thomas le Despenser</a>, who became <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Earl of Gloucester">Earl of Gloucester</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Enone"><a href="#endnote_Enone">[e]</a></sup> With the forfeited lands of the convicted appellants, the king could reward these men with lands suited to their new ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-M483-4_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M483-4-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>A threat to Richard's authority still existed, however, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lancaster" title="House of Lancaster">House of Lancaster</a>, represented by John of Gaunt and his son Henry Bolingbroke, Duke of Hereford. The House of Lancaster not only possessed greater wealth than any other family in England, they were of royal descent and, as such, likely candidates to succeed the childless Richard.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Discord broke out in the inner circles of court in December 1397, when Bolingbroke <sup id="cite_ref-M483-4_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-M483-4-64">[64]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_de_Mowbray,_1st_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas de Mowbray, 1st Duke of Norfolk">Mowbray</a> became embroiled in a quarrel. According to Bolingbroke, Mowbray had claimed that the two, as former Lords Appellant, were next in line for royal retribution. Mowbray vehemently denied these charges, as such a claim would have amounted to treason.<sup id="cite_ref-Saul_.282005.29.2C_p._63._63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saul_.282005.29.2C_p._63.-63">[63]</a></sup> A parliamentary committee decided that the two should settle the matter by battle, but at the last moment Richard exiled the two dukes instead: Mowbray for life, Bolingbroke for ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> On 3 February 1399, John of Gaunt died. Rather than allowing Bolingbroke to succeed, Richard extended the term of his exile to life and expropriated his properties.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The king felt safe from Bolingbroke, who was residing in Paris, since the French had little interest in any challenge to Richard and his peace policy.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Richard left the country in May for another expedition in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1398 Richard summoned a packed Parliament to Shrewsbury—known as the Parliament of Shrewsbury—which declared all the acts of the Merciless Parliament to be null and void, and announced that no restraint could legally be put on the king. It delegated all parliamentary power to a committee of twelve lords and six commoners chosen from the king's friends, making Richard an absolute ruler unbound by the necessity of gathering a Parliament again.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overthrow_and_death">Overthrow and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Overthrow and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_II_arrest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Richard_II_arrest.jpg/170px-Richard_II_arrest.jpg" width="170" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Richard_II_arrest.jpg/255px-Richard_II_arrest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Richard_II_arrest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="347" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Richard_II_arrest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Richard being taken into custody by the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_1st_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland">Earl of Northumberland</a> (Froissart)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In June 1399, <a href="/wiki/Louis_I_de_Valois,_Duke_of_Orl%C3%A9ans" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis I de Valois, Duke of Orléans">Louis, Duke of Orléans</a>, gained control of the court of the insane <a href="/wiki/Charles_VI_of_France" title="Charles VI of France">Charles VI of France</a>. The policy of <i>rapprochement</i> with the English crown did not suit Louis's political ambitions, and for this reason he found it opportune to allow Henry to leave for England.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> With a small group of followers, Bolingbroke landed at <a href="/wiki/Ravenspur" class="mw-redirect" title="Ravenspur">Ravenspur</a> in Yorkshire towards the end of June 1399.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Men from all over the country soon rallied around the duke. Meeting with <a href="/wiki/Henry_Percy,_1st_Earl_of_Northumberland" title="Henry Percy, 1st Earl of Northumberland">Henry Percy, Earl of Northumberland</a>, who had his own misgivings about the king, Bolingbroke insisted that his only object was to regain his own patrimony. Percy took him at his word and declined to interfere.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The king had taken most of his household knights and the loyal members of his nobility with him to Ireland, so Henry experienced little resistance as he moved south. <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Langley,_1st_Duke_of_York" title="Edmund of Langley, 1st Duke of York">Edmund of Langley, Duke of York</a>, who was acting as Keeper of the Realm, had little choice but to side with Bolingbroke.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Richard was delayed in his return from Ireland and did not land in Wales until 24 July.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> He made his way to <a href="/wiki/Conwy_Castle" title="Conwy Castle">Conwy</a>, where on 12 August he met with the Earl of Northumberland for negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> On 19 August, Richard II surrendered to Henry at <a href="/wiki/Flint_Castle" title="Flint Castle">Flint Castle</a>, promising to abdicate if his life were spared.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Both men then returned to London, the indignant king riding all the way behind Henry. On arrival, he was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a> on 1 September.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png/300px-Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png" width="300" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png/450px-Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png/600px-Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png 2x" data-file-width="3784" data-file-height="3212" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bolingbroke-richard-flint-castle-harley-ms-1319.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Richard's surrender to Henry at Flint Castle, <a href="/wiki/Harleian_Collection" class="mw-redirect" title="Harleian Collection">Harleian Collection</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Henry was by now fully determined to take the throne, but presenting a rationale for this action proved a dilemma.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> It was argued that Richard, through his tyranny and misgovernment, had rendered himself unworthy of being king.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, Henry was not next in line to the throne; the heir presumptive was <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Mortimer,_5th_Earl_of_March" title="Edmund Mortimer, 5th Earl of March">Edmund Mortimer, Earl of March</a>, who was descended from <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>'s third son, the second to survive to adulthood, <a href="/wiki/Lionel_of_Antwerp,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="Lionel of Antwerp, 1st Duke of Clarence">Lionel of Antwerp</a>. Bolingbroke's father, John of Gaunt, was Edward's fourth son, the third to survive to adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The problem was solved by emphasising Henry's descent in a direct <i>male</i> line, whereas March's descent was through his grandmother.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Fnone"><a href="#endnote_Fnone">[f]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the official record (read by Thomas Arundel, archbishop of Canterbury, during an assembly of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords#History" title="House of Lords">lords</a> and commons at Westminster Hall on Tuesday 30 September), Richard gave up his crown willingly and ratified his deposition citing as a reason his own unworthiness as a monarch. On the other hand, the <i>Traison et Mort Chronicle</i> suggests otherwise. It describes a meeting between Richard and Henry that took place one day before the parliament's session. The king succumbed to blind rage, ordered his release from the Tower, called his cousin a traitor, demanded to see his <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_Valois" title="Isabella of Valois">wife</a> and swore revenge throwing down his bonnet, while the duke refused to do anything without parliamentary approval.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>When parliament met to discuss Richard's fate, the <a href="/wiki/John_Trevor_(died_1410)" title="John Trevor (died 1410)">bishop of St Asaph</a> read thirty-three articles of deposition that were unanimously accepted by lords and commons. On 1 October 1399, Richard II was formally deposed and on 13 October, the feast day of <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, Henry Bolingbroke, Duke of Lancaster, was crowned Henry IV of England.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exact course of Richard's life after the deposition is unclear; he remained in the Tower until he was taken to <a href="/wiki/Pontefract_Castle" title="Pontefract Castle">Pontefract Castle</a> shortly before the end of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Although King Henry might have been amenable to letting him live, this all changed when it was revealed that the earls of Huntingdon, Kent and Salisbury and Lord Despenser, and possibly also the Earl of Rutland&#160;– all now demoted from the ranks they had been given by Richard&#160;– were planning to murder the new king and restore Richard in the <a href="/wiki/Epiphany_Rising" title="Epiphany Rising">Epiphany Rising</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Although averted, the plot highlighted the danger of allowing Richard to live. He is thought to have starved to death in captivity on or around 14 February 1400, although there is some question over the date and manner of his death.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> His body was taken south from Pontefract and displayed in the <a href="/wiki/Old_St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="Old St Paul's Cathedral">old St Paul's Cathedral</a> on 17 February before burial in <a href="/wiki/Kings_Langley" title="Kings Langley">Kings Langley</a> Church on 6 March.</p>
<p>Rumours that Richard was still alive persisted, but never gained much credence in England;<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> in Scotland, however, a man identified as Richard came into the hands of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stewart,_Duke_of_Albany" title="Robert Stewart, Duke of Albany">Regent Albany</a>, lodged in <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a>, and serving as the notional&#160;– and perhaps reluctant&#160;– figurehead of various anti-Lancastrian and <a href="/wiki/Lollard" class="mw-redirect" title="Lollard">Lollard</a> intrigues in England. Henry IV's government dismissed him as an imposter, and several sources from both sides of the Border suggest the man had a mental illness, one also describing him as a "beggar" by the time of his death in 1419, but he was buried as a king in the local <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> friary in Stirling. Meanwhile, in 1413, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>&#160;– in an effort both to atone for his father's act of murder and to silence the rumours of Richard's survival&#160;– had decided to have the body at King's Langley moved to its final resting place in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. Here Richard himself had prepared an elaborate tomb, where the remains of his wife Anne were already entombed.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Court_culture">Court culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Court culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><b><a href="/wiki/Wilton_Diptych" title="Wilton Diptych">Wilton Diptych</a></b></div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilton_diptych.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wilton_diptych.jpg/200px-Wilton_diptych.jpg" width="200" height="156" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wilton_diptych.jpg/300px-Wilton_diptych.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wilton_diptych.jpg/400px-Wilton_diptych.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2992" data-file-height="2336" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Richard venerating the Virgin and Child, accompanied by his patron saints: <a href="/wiki/Edmund_the_Martyr" title="Edmund the Martyr">Edmund the Martyr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> (<i>l.</i> to <i>r.</i>)</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Wilton_diptych2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Wilton_diptych2.jpg/200px-Wilton_diptych2.jpg" width="200" height="156" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Wilton_diptych2.jpg/300px-Wilton_diptych2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Wilton_diptych2.jpg/400px-Wilton_diptych2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1270" data-file-height="988" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Richard's arms of Edward the Confessor's mythical arms impaled with those of the Kings of England (<i>l.</i>) and Richard's white hart <a href="/wiki/Emblem" title="Emblem">emblem</a> (<i>r.</i>)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the last years of Richard's reign, and particularly in the months after the suppression of the appellants in 1397, the king enjoyed a virtual monopoly on power in the country, a relatively uncommon situation in medieval England.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> In this period a particular court culture was allowed to emerge, one that differed sharply from that of earlier times. A new form of address developed; where the king previously had been addressed simply as "<a href="/wiki/Highness" title="Highness">highness</a>", now "royal <a href="/wiki/Majesty" title="Majesty">majesty</a>", or "high majesty" were often used. It was said that on solemn festivals Richard would sit on his throne in the royal hall for hours without speaking, and anyone on whom his eyes fell had to bow his knees to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The inspiration for this new sumptuousness and emphasis on dignity came from the courts on the continent, not only the French and Bohemian courts that had been the homes of Richard's two wives, but also the court that the Black Prince had maintained while residing in Aquitaine.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard's approach to kingship was rooted in his strong belief in the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Prerogative" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Prerogative">royal prerogative</a>, the inspiration of which can be found in his early youth, when his authority was challenged first by the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants' Revolt">Peasants' Revolts</a> and then by the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Richard rejected the approach his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, had taken to the nobility. Edward's court had been a martial one, based on the interdependence between the king and his most trusted noblemen as military captains.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In Richard's view, this put a dangerous amount of power in the hands of the baronage. To avoid dependence on the nobility for military recruitment, he pursued a policy of peace towards France.<sup id="cite_ref-S439_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S439-92">[92]</a></sup> At the same time, he developed his own private military retinue, larger than that of any English king before him, and gave them <a href="/wiki/Livery" title="Livery">livery</a> <a href="/wiki/Heraldic_badge" title="Heraldic badge">badges</a> with his <a href="/wiki/White_Hart" title="White Hart">White Hart</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> which are also worn by the angels in the Wilton Diptych (right). He was then free to develop a courtly atmosphere in which the king was a distant, venerated figure, and art and culture, rather than warfare, were at the centre.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Patronage_and_the_arts">Patronage and the arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Patronage and the arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As part of Richard's programme of asserting his authority, he also tried to cultivate the royal image. Unlike any other English king before him, he had himself portrayed in <a href="/wiki/Panel_painting" title="Panel painting">panel paintings</a> of elevated majesty,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> of which two survive: the over life-size <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> portrait of the king (c.&#160;1390, see top of page), and the <a href="/wiki/Wilton_Diptych" title="Wilton Diptych">Wilton Diptych</a> (1394–99), a portable work probably intended to accompany Richard on his Irish campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> It is one of the few surviving English examples of the courtly <a href="/wiki/International_Gothic" title="International Gothic">International Gothic</a> style of painting that was developed in the courts of the Continent, especially Prague and Paris.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Richard's expenditure on jewellery, rich textiles and metalwork was far higher than on paintings, but as with his <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>, there are hardly any surviving works that can be connected with him, except for a crown, "one of the finest achievements of the Gothic goldsmith", that probably belonged to Anne.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg/220px-Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg/330px-Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg/440px-Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg 2x" data-file-width="633" data-file-height="860" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Westminster_Hall_edited.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Westminster_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Hall">Westminster Hall</a> in the early 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Among Richard's grandest projects in the field of architecture was <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Hall">Westminster Hall</a>, which was extensively rebuilt during his reign,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> perhaps spurred on by the completion in 1391 of John of Gaunt's magnificent hall at <a href="/wiki/Kenilworth_Castle" title="Kenilworth Castle">Kenilworth Castle</a>. Fifteen life-size statues of kings were placed in niches on the walls, and the <a href="/wiki/Hammer-beam" class="mw-redirect" title="Hammer-beam">hammer-beam</a> roof by the royal carpenter <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Herland" title="Hugh Herland">Hugh Herland</a>, "the greatest creation of medieval timber architecture", allowed the original three <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> aisles to be replaced with a single huge open space, with a dais at the end for Richard to sit in solitary state.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The rebuilding had been begun by <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a> in 1245, but had by Richard's time been dormant for over a century.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The court's patronage of literature is especially important, because this was the period in which the English language took shape as a <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_literature#Late_period" title="Middle English literature">literary language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> There is little evidence to tie Richard directly to patronage of <a href="/wiki/English_poetry#The_Anglo-Norman_period_and_the_Later_Middle_Ages" title="English poetry">poetry</a>, but it was nevertheless within his court that this culture was allowed to thrive.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The greatest poet of the age, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, served the king as a diplomat, a customs official and a clerk of <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Works" title="Office of Works">The King's Works</a> while producing some of his best-known work.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Chaucer was also in the service of John of Gaunt, and wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_Duchess" title="The Book of the Duchess">The Book of the Duchess</a></i> as a eulogy to Gaunt's wife <a href="/wiki/Blanche_of_Lancaster" title="Blanche of Lancaster">Blanche</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Chaucer's colleague and friend <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">John Gower</a> wrote his <i><a href="/wiki/Confessio_Amantis" title="Confessio Amantis">Confessio Amantis</a></i> on a direct commission from Richard, although he later grew disenchanted with the king.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character_and_assessment">Character and assessment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Character and assessment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Contemporary writers, even those less sympathetic to the king, agreed that Richard was a "most beautiful king", though with a "face which was white, rounded and feminine", implying he lacked manliness.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> He was athletic and tall; when his tomb was opened in 1871 he was found to be six feet tall.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> He was also intelligent and well read, and when agitated he had a tendency to stammer.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> While the Westminster Abbey portrait probably shows a good similarity of the king, the Wilton Diptych portrays the king as significantly younger than he was at the time; it must be assumed that he had a beard by this point.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Religiously, he was orthodox, and particularly towards the end of his reign he became a strong opponent of the <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollard</a> heresy.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> He was particularly devoted to the cult of Edward the Confessor, and around 1395 he had his own <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">coat of arms</a> <a href="/wiki/Impalement_(heraldry)" title="Impalement (heraldry)">impaled</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Attributed_arms" title="Attributed arms">mythical arms</a> of the Confessor.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> Though not a warrior king like his grandfather, Richard nevertheless enjoyed <a href="/wiki/Tournament_(medieval)" title="Tournament (medieval)">tournaments</a>, as well as hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Richard_II_from_NPG_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/220px-King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/330px-King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg/440px-King_Richard_II_from_NPG_%282%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="3164" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:King_Richard_II_from_NPG_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anonymous artist's impression of Richard II in the 16th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The popular view of Richard has more than anything been influenced by <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a>'s play about the king, <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">Richard II</a></i>. Shakespeare's Richard was a cruel, vindictive and irresponsible king, who attained a semblance of greatness only after his fall from power.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Writing a work of fiction, however, Shakespeare took many liberties and made great omissions. Shakespeare based his play on works by writers such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Hall" title="Edward Hall">Edward Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Daniel" title="Samuel Daniel">Samuel Daniel</a>, who in turn based their writings on contemporary chroniclers such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Walsingham" title="Thomas Walsingham">Thomas Walsingham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Hall and Daniel were part of <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor</a> historiography, which was highly unsympathetic to Richard.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The Tudor orthodoxy, reinforced by Shakespeare, saw a continuity in civil discord starting with Richard's misrule that did not end until <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>'s accession in 1485.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> The idea that Richard was to blame for the later-15th century <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a> was prevalent as late as the 19th century, but came to be challenged in the 20th.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Some recent historians prefer to look at the Wars of the Roses in isolation from the reign of Richard II.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Richard's mental state has been a major issue of historical debate since the first academic historians started treating the subject in the 19th century. One of the first modern historians to deal with Richard II as a king and as a person was Bishop <a href="/wiki/William_Stubbs" title="William Stubbs">Stubbs</a>. Stubbs argued that towards the end of his reign, Richard's mind "was losing its balance altogether".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Steel_(historian)" title="Anthony Steel (historian)">Anthony Steel</a>, who wrote a full-scale biography of the king in 1941, took a psychiatric approach to the issue, and concluded that the king had <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> This was challenged by <a href="/wiki/Vivian_Hunter_Galbraith" title="Vivian Hunter Galbraith">V. H. Galbraith</a>, who argued that there was no historical basis for such a diagnosis,<sup id="cite_ref-Galbraith_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galbraith-121">[121]</a></sup> a line that has also been followed by later historians of the period, such as <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Goodman_(historian)" title="Anthony Goodman (historian)">Anthony Goodman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Tuck" title="Anthony Tuck">Anthony Tuck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Saul" title="Nigel Saul">Nigel Saul</a>, who wrote the most recent academic biography on Richard II, concedes that&#160;– even though there is no basis for assuming the king had a mental illness&#160;– he showed clear signs of a <a href="/wiki/Narcissistic_personality_disorder" title="Narcissistic personality disorder">narcissistic personality</a>, and towards the end of his reign "Richard's grasp on reality was becoming weaker".<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the primary historiographical questions surrounding Richard concerns his political agenda and the reasons for its failure. His kingship was thought to contain elements of the early modern <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a> as exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> More recently, Richard's concept of kingship has been seen by some as not so different from that of his antecedents, and that it was exactly by staying within the framework of traditional monarchy that he was able to achieve as much as he did.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-W63_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W63-124">[124]</a></sup> Yet his actions were too extreme, and too abrupt. For one, the absence of war was meant to reduce the burden of taxation, and so help Richard's popularity with the Commons in parliament. However, this promise was never fulfilled, as the cost of the royal retinue, the opulence of court and Richard's lavish patronage of his favourites proved as expensive as war had been, without offering commensurate benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-S439_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-S439-92">[92]</a></sup> As for his policy of military retaining, this was later emulated by <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward IV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>, but Richard's exclusive reliance on the county of Cheshire hurt his support from the rest of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> As <a href="/wiki/Simon_Walker_(historian)" title="Simon Walker (historian)">Simon Walker</a> concludes: "What he sought was, in contemporary terms, neither unjustified nor unattainable; it was the manner of his seeking that betrayed him."<sup id="cite_ref-W63_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W63-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ancestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
