<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Richard_II_of_England" title="Cultural depictions of Richard II of England">Cultural depictions of Richard II of England</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_II_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
<p><b>a.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Anone"><a href="#ref_Anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> John of Gaunt's brother <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Langley,_1st_Duke_of_York" title="Edmund of Langley, 1st Duke of York">Edmund of Langley</a> was only one year younger, but it has been suggested that this prince was of "limited ability", and he took less part in government than Gaunt did.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><br />
<b>b.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Bnone"><a href="#ref_Bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> It has been speculated that the whole incident surrounding the killing of Wat Tyler was in fact planned in advance by the council, in order to end the rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><br />
<b>c.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Cnone"><a href="#ref_Cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> While both England and the Empire supported <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VI" title="Pope Urban VI">Pope Urban VI</a> in Rome, the French sided with the <a href="/wiki/Avignon_Papacy" title="Avignon Papacy">Avignon Papacy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antipope_Clement_VII" title="Antipope Clement VII">Clement VII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup><br />
<b>d.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Dnone"><a href="#ref_Dnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> This "appeal"&#160;– which would give its name to the <a href="/wiki/Lords_Appellant" title="Lords Appellant">Lords Appellant</a>&#160;– was not an appeal in the modern sense of an application to a higher authority. In medieval <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> the appeal was criminal charge, often one of treason.<sup id="cite_ref-Tuck_3-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tuck-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><br />
<b>e.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Enone"><a href="#ref_Enone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Beaufort was the oldest of John of Gaunt's children with <a href="/wiki/Katherine_Swynford" title="Katherine Swynford">Katherine Swynford</a>; illegitimate children whom Richard had given legitimate status in 1390. He was made <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Dorset" title="Marquess of Dorset">Marquess of Dorset</a>; <a href="/wiki/Marquess" title="Marquess">marquess</a> being a relatively new title in England up until this point. Rutland, heir to the <a href="/wiki/Edmund_of_Langley,_1st_Duke_of_York" title="Edmund of Langley, 1st Duke of York">Duke of York</a>, was created <a href="/wiki/Counts_and_Dukes_of_Aumale" class="mw-redirect" title="Counts and Dukes of Aumale">Duke of Aumale</a>. Montacute had succeeded <a href="/wiki/William_Montacute,_2nd_Earl_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="William Montacute, 2nd Earl of Salisbury">his uncle</a> as <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Earl of Salisbury">Earl of Salisbury</a> earlier the same year. Despenser, the great-grandson of <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Despenser_the_Younger" title="Hugh Despenser the Younger">Hugh Despenser the Younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a>'s favourite who was executed for treason in 1326, was given the forfeited <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Gloucester" title="Earl of Gloucester">earldom of Gloucester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><br />
<b>f.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Fnone"><a href="#ref_Fnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Though it had become established tradition for earldoms to descend in the male line, there was no such tradition for royal <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_British_throne" title="Succession to the British throne">succession in England</a>. The precedence could indeed be seen to invalidate the English claim to the French throne, based on succession through the female line, over which the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years' War">Hundred Years' War</a> was being fought.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
</div>
